<link rel="schema.DC" href="http://purl.org/DC/elements/1.0/" /><meta name="dc.Title" content="Crystalline Nanochannels with Pendant Azobenzene Groups: Steric or Polar Effects on Gas Adsorption and Diffusion?" /><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Hubiao Huang" /><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Hiroshi Sato" /><meta name="dc.Creator" content="Takuzo Aida" /><meta name="dc.Description" content="An azobenzene-containing, zirconium-based metal–organic framework (AzoMOF), upon irradiation with ultraviolet (UV) light at 365 ± 10 nm, underwent trans-to-cis isomerization of its azobenzene pendants to furnish the cis-isomer content of 21% (AzoMOF21%) in 30 min at the photostationary state and underwent backward isomerization into AzoMOF1% upon either irradiation with visible light (420–480 nm) or heating. When the cis-isomer content increased, the diffusion rate and amount of CO2 adsorbed into the nanochannels of AzoMOF decreased considerably. When erythrosine B, a polarity-probing guest, was used, it showed a red shift upon exposure of AzoMOF20%⊃EB to visible light, indicating that the interior environment of AzoMOF turns less polar as the trans-isomer content becomes higher. In sharp contrast, the adsorption profiles of AzoMOF15% and AzoMOF1% for Ar having an analogous kinetic diameter to CO2 but no quadrupole moment and a smaller polarizability were virtually identical to one another. Therefore, it ..." /><meta name="dc.Description" content="" /><meta name="dc.Publisher" content="American Chemical Society" /><meta name="dc.Date" scheme="WTN8601" content="June 26, 2017" /><meta name="dc.Type" content="rapid-communication" /><meta name="dc.Format" content="text/HTML" /><meta name="dc.Identifier" scheme="doi" content="10.1021/jacs.7b02979" /><meta name="dc.Language" content="EN" /><meta name="dc.Coverage" content="world" /><meta name="citation_fulltext_world_readable" content=""/>

	var journalTitle = 'JACS';

	var searchTerms = [];

	if (journalTitle != '$' + '{journal.dartId}') {
		if (articleKeyword.length > 0) {
			$('.keywords').children('.keyword').each(function() {
				searchTerms.push($(this).text().toString());
			});

		else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
			$(articleDescription).each(function() {
				articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
				articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
				articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
				searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
			});
		}

		if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
			searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
		}
	}

	googletag.cmd.push(function() {
		if (journalTitle == '$' + '{journal.dartId}') {
			googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/default/article', [[970, 90], [728, 90]], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').addService(googletag.pubads()).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1');

		else {
			googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/JACS/article', [[970, 90], [728, 90]], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').addService(googletag.pubads()).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1');
		}

		googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
		googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
		googletag.enableServices();
	});
	displayAd = $('<script>', {'type': 'text/javascript'});
	$(displayAd).append("googletag.cmd.push(function() { googletag.display('dfp-journal-article-leaderboard'); });");
	$('#dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').append(displayAd);
});

$(function(){
	$('#nav-pubs-clicklayer').hover(
		function() {
			hideAZ = setTimeout(function() {
				$('#nav-pubs').removeClass('showAzList showEbooksList');
				$('#nav-pubs-clicklayer').toggle();
			}, 1000);
		}, function() {
			clearTimeout(hideAZ);
		}
	).click(function(){
		$('#nav-pubs').removeClass('showAzList showEbooksList');
		$('#nav-pubs-clicklayer').toggle();
	});
});
function showAZList() {
	$('#nav-pubs').toggleClass('showAzList');
	$('#nav-pubs-clicklayer').toggle();
}
$(function(){
	$(".ebooks-link").click(function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		$('#nav-pubs').toggleClass('showEbooksList');
		$('#nav-pubs-clicklayer').toggle();
	});
});
</script>

            <div class="widget-body body body-none  body-compact-all"><script type="text/javascript">
function toggleSubjects(li) {
	if ($(li).hasClass("open")) {
		$(li).removeClass("open");
	}
	else {
		$(li).parent().find('.category').removeClass("open");
		$(li).addClass("open");
	}
	return false;
}
$(function(){
	$('#qs-subjects .close-tab').click(function() {
		$('.qs-tabs .tab-nav a').first().click();
	});
});
</script>

    if (dbid != anyDbId && (140737488355327 & dbid) == 0) return;

    }

    return popRefImpl(rid, citart, ptype, area, 400, 100);
}

    var doi = "10.1021%2Fjacs.7b02979";
    if (! citart) { citart = "citart1"; }
    if (! ptype) { ptype = ""; }
    var pt = rid.charAt(0) + "jacs7b02979" + ptype;
    return popupRef(citart, rid, doi, pt, area, width, height);
}

        <div class="tab-content ">
            <div class="tab tab-pane active">

                        <article class="article">
                                <h1 class="articleTitle"><span class="hlFld-Title">Crystalline Nanochannels with Pendant Azobenzene Groups: Steric or Polar Effects on Gas Adsorption and Diffusion?</span></h1><div id="articleMeta"><div id="authors"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"><a id="authors" href="/author/Huang%2C+Hubiao">Hubiao Huang</a></span><span><span class="NLM_xref-aff"><sup>†</sup></span></span><span>, </span></span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"><a id="authors" href="/author/Sato%2C+Hiroshi">Hiroshi Sato</a></span><span><a class="ref" href="#cor1"><sup>*</sup></a></span><span><span class="NLM_xref-aff"><sup>†</sup></span></span><span><span class="orcid-link-icon"> <a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5899-1340" target="_blank"><img src="/templates/jsp/images/orcid.png" /></a></span>, and </span></span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor"><a id="authors" href="/author/Aida%2C+Takuzo">Takuzo Aida</a></span><span><a class="ref" href="#cor2"><sup>*</sup></a></span><span><span class="NLM_xref-aff"><sup>†</sup></span></span><span><span class="NLM_xref-aff"><sup>‡</sup></span></span><span> </span><span class="orcid-link-icon"> <a href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-0002-8017" target="_blank"><img src="/templates/jsp/images/orcid.png" /></a></span></span></div><div class="affiliations"><div id="aff1"><sup>†</sup> Department
of Chemistry and Biotechnology, School of Engineering, <span class="institution">The University of Tokyo</span>, 7-3-1 Hongo, Bunkyo-ku, Tokyo 113-8656, <span class="country">Japan</span></div><div id="aff2"><sup>‡</sup> <span class="institution">RIKEN
Center for Emergent Matter Science</span>, 2-1 Hirosawa, Wako, Saitama 351-0198, <span class="country">Japan</span></div></div><div id="citation"><cite>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</cite>, Article ASAP</div><div id="doi"><strong>DOI: </strong>10.1021/jacs.7b02979</div><div id="pubDate">Publication Date (Web): June 21, 2017</div><div id="artCopyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div id="correspondence">*<a href="mailto:hsato@macro.t.u-tokyo.ac.jp">hsato@macro.t.u-tokyo.ac.jp</a>, *<a href="mailto:aida@macro.t.u-tokyo.ac.jp">aida@macro.t.u-tokyo.ac.jp</a></div></div><div id="articleBody"><div class="hlFld-Abstract"><ul class="anchors"><li><a title="Jump to a Section" onclick="showPulldown(this); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="anchorIMG"></a><ul style="display: none;"><li><a onclick="hidePulldown(this);" title="Top of Page" href="#top"><span>Top of Page</span></a></li><li><a onclick="hidePulldown(this);" title="Abstract" href="#Abstract"><span>Abstract</span></a></li><li><a onclick="hidePulldown(this);" title="References" href="#_i3"><span>References</span></a></li></ul></li></ul><h2 id="Abstract">Abstract</h2><div id="abstractBox"><div class="figure" id="f_tgr1"><a title="Open Figure Viewer" href="#" class="showFiguresEvent" id="absImg"><img alt="Abstract Image" src="/appl/literatum/publisher/achs/journals/content/jacsat/0/jacsat.ahead-of-print/jacs.7b02979/20170626/images/medium/ja-2017-02979y_0005.gif" /><span class="overlay"></span></a></div><p class="articleBody_abstractText">An azobenzene-containing, zirconium-based metal–organic framework (<sup>Azo</sup>MOF), upon irradiation with ultraviolet (UV) light at 365 ± 10 nm, underwent <i>trans</i>-to-<i>cis</i> isomerization of its azobenzene pendants to furnish the <i>cis</i>-isomer content of 21% (<sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>21%</sup>) in 30 min at the photostationary state and underwent backward isomerization into <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> upon either irradiation with visible light (420–480 nm) or heating. When the <i>cis</i>-isomer content increased, the diffusion rate and amount of CO<sub>2</sub> adsorbed into the nanochannels of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF decreased considerably. When erythrosine B, a polarity-probing guest, was used, it showed a red shift upon exposure of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup>⊃EB to visible light, indicating that the interior environment of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF turns less polar as the <i>trans</i>-isomer content becomes higher. In sharp contrast, the adsorption profiles of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> for Ar having an analogous kinetic diameter to CO<sub>2</sub> but no quadrupole moment and a smaller polarizability were virtually identical to one another. Therefore, it is likely that CO<sub>2</sub> experiences a dominant effect of a polar effect rather than a steric effect in the crystalline nanochannels.</p></div></div><div class="hlFld-Fulltext"><p class="first">The integration of photoresponsive building blocks, such as azobenzene,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(1)</a> diarylethene,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(2)</a> aryl azide,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(3)</a> and nitrobenzyl moieties,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(4)</a> into metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) is a leading strategy for realizing remote or noninvasive manipulation of their functions.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(5)</a> Azobenzene derivatives are thoroughly investigated photoswitches that provide large geometrical changes based on their photochemical isomerization with ultraviolet (UV) and visible light. This isomerization could also provide a remarkable alteration in polarity because the dipole moments of the <i>trans</i>- and <i>cis</i>-isomers of nonsubstituted azobenzene are approximately zero and 3.1 D, respectively.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(6)</a> Although several pioneering works on azobenzene-appended MOFs have been published so far,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref7'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(1, 7)</a> only one group claimed a polar effect based on an observation by means of quartz crystal microbalance that a MOF enhances its affinity toward butanol and butanediol vapors to some extent (5–20%) when it is exposed to UV light for inducing the <i>trans</i>-to-<i>cis</i> isomerization of the azobenzene moieties.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(7)</a> However, to the best of our knowledge, no claim was given to how the polarity change of the channel interior upon azobenzene isomerization in MOFs affects the diffusion kinetics of gaseous molecules.</p><p>Here, we report <sup>Azo</sup>MOF, a zirconium-based MOF<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(8)</a> with azobenzene pendants (Zr<sub>6</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>4</sub><i>L</i><sub>6</sub>; <i>L</i><sup>2–</sup> = 2′-phenyldiazenyl-1,1′:4′,1″-terphenyl-4,4″-dicarboxylate, <a class="ref" href="#fig1">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig1">1</a></a>a) and its adsorption/diffusion features of gaseous molecules CO<sub>2</sub> and Ar, in response to the photoisomerization of the azobenzene pendants. In particular, we highlight that the isomerization unprecedentedly exerts a significant effect on the diffusion kinetics of CO<sub>2</sub> but not on that of Ar. These contrasting results are highly interesting, considering that the kinetic diameters of CO<sub>2</sub> (3.3 Å) and Ar (3.4 Å) are substantially identical to one another and that CO<sub>2</sub> has a quadrupole moment (−14.9 × 10<sup>–40</sup> C m<sup>2</sup>) and a higher polarizability (29.1 × 10<sup>25</sup> cm<sup>3</sup>) than Ar (16.4 × 10<sup>25</sup> cm<sup>3</sup>), although both of CO<sub>2</sub> and Ar are apolar.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(9)</a> Erythrosine B, used as a polarity-sensitive guest molecule, allowed us to confirm that the nanochannels of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF turn more polar as their <i>cis</i>-isomer content increases.</p><div class="figure" id="fig1"><a title="Open Figure Viewer" href="#" class="thumbnail showFiguresEvent"><img alt="figure" src="/appl/literatum/publisher/achs/journals/content/jacsat/0/jacsat.ahead-of-print/jacs.7b02979/20170626/images/small/ja-2017-02979y_0001.gif" /><span class="overlay"></span></a><div class="caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic representation of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF with azobenzene (Azo) groups as photoisomerizable pendants. The Azo groups adopting a <i>cis</i> configuration are highlighted with purple-colored circles. (b) Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) pattern of guest-free <sup>Azo</sup>MOF (red) and a simulated pattern for as-synthesized <sup>Azo</sup>MOF (black). The inset in panel b shows an optical image of a single crystal of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup>. (c) <sup>1</sup>H NMR evaluation of the <i>trans</i>-to-<i>cis</i> isomerization in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF upon irradiation with UV light (365 ± 10 nm) at 25 °C.</p></div></div><p>Prior to the synthesis of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF (<a class="ref" href="#fig1">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig1">1</a></a>a), we confirmed that the azobenzene pendant of its terphenylene ligand (<sup>Azo</sup><i>L</i>H<sub>2</sub>) itself undergoes reversible photochemical isomerization in DMSO (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4 and S5</a>). <sup>Azo</sup>MOF was synthesized by a reaction between <sup>Azo</sup><i>L</i>H<sub>2</sub> (0.05 mmol) and ZrCl<sub>4</sub> (0.05 mmol) in DMF (2 mL) containing a mixture of benzoic acid (1.5 mmol) and water (2 μL)<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(10)</a> at 120 °C (see <a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Single crystals suitable for X-ray diffraction analysis were obtained, whereas powdery crystalline samples for spectroscopic and adsorption studies were prepared by pulverizing the single crystals (<a class="ref" href="#fig1">Figures <a class="ref" href="#fig1">1</a></a>b and <a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>). The crystal structure (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8 and Table S1</a>) revealed that <sup>Azo</sup>MOF consists of small tetrahedral and large octahedral pores (<a class="ref" href="#fig2">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig2">2</a></a>a), which are connected only heterotropically by sharing a triangular window (<a class="ref" href="#fig2">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig2">2</a></a>b). Diffuse reflectance spectroscopy of guest-free <sup>Azo</sup>MOF upon irradiation with UV light at 365 ± 10 nm clearly showed a continuous enhancement of the n−π* transition band with the irradiation time (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a</a>), indicating the occurrence of <i>trans</i>-to-<i>cis</i> isomerization of the azobenzene pendants in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF. Upon subsequent irradiation of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF with visible light at 420–480 nm, the azobenzene pendants isomerized back to the <i>trans</i> form (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>). These forward and backward isomerization events could be repeated multiple times without any noticeable fatigue and structural failure (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S12 and S13</a>). The <i>cis</i>-azobenzene unit, attached to the pillar linker, showed a sufficiently high thermal stability at ambient temperatures with a half-life (τ<sub>1/2</sub>) of 10 days (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). By taking advantage of this geometrical stability, we successfully obtained the time-dependent isomerization profile of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF (<a class="ref" href="#fig1">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig1">1</a></a>c) by digesting the irradiated samples with DCl in DMSO-<i>d</i><sub>6</sub> to recover <sup>Azo</sup><i>L</i>H<sub>2</sub> (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). The result indicates that the photostationary state upon UV irradiation is established in 30 min with a <i>cis</i>-isomer content of 21%.</p><div class="figure" id="fig2"><a title="Open Figure Viewer" href="#" class="thumbnail showFiguresEvent"><img alt="figure" src="/appl/literatum/publisher/achs/journals/content/jacsat/0/jacsat.ahead-of-print/jacs.7b02979/20170626/images/small/ja-2017-02979y_0002.gif" /><span class="overlay"></span></a><div class="caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Single-crystal X-ray structures of tetrahedral and octahedral pores in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF. (b) Schematic illustration of the pore structures in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF. The tetrahedral and octahedral pores are highlighted in purple and green colors, respectively.</p></div></div><p>We investigated the adsorption profiles of CO<sub>2</sub> and Ar in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (<i>cis</i>% = 1%) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (<i>cis</i>% = 15%), the latter of which was prepared by 30 min UV (365 ± 10 nm) irradiation of powdery <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (30 mg) sandwiched between two glass plates as illustrated in <a class="ref" href="#fig1">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig1">1</a></a>c (inset). Just like other zirconium-based MOFs with two different-sized pores,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(11)</a> both <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue) showed a stepwise adsorption isotherm for CO<sub>2</sub> at 195 K with a clear inflection point at <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0.15 for the secondary adsorption to occur (<i>P</i> = equilibrium vapor pressure, <i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = saturated vapor pressure) (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>a). According to the reported examples,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(11)</a> the primary and secondary adsorption features in <a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>a are assignable to the pore-filling processes for the tetrahedral and octahedral pores with CO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The important observation here was a marked decrease in the amount of adsorbed CO<sub>2</sub> upon photochemical conversion of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) into <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue). For Ar, a stepwise adsorption isotherm was likewise observed for <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> at 87 K (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>b), though in a much narrower <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> range, with an inflection point at <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0.01 (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>b, inset). However, in contrast to the case of CO<sub>2</sub>, the adsorption profile of Ar barely changed upon photochemical conversion of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) into <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue). Accordingly, the pore size distribution (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) and Brunauer–Emmet–Teller (BET) surface areas, evaluated with Ar for <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (1412 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (1418 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), were substantially identical to one another.</p><div class="figure" id="fig3"><a title="Open Figure Viewer" href="#" class="thumbnail showFiguresEvent"><img alt="figure" src="/appl/literatum/publisher/achs/journals/content/jacsat/0/jacsat.ahead-of-print/jacs.7b02979/20170626/images/small/ja-2017-02979y_0003.gif" /><span class="overlay"></span></a><div class="caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Adsorption isotherms of (a) CO<sub>2</sub> at 195 K and (b) Ar at 87 K in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue), respectively, where the inflection points for the secondary adsorption to start appear at <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0.15 and 0.01, respectively. The inset in panel b shows adsorption isotherms of Ar enlarged in a range of <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> from 0 to 0.06. Diffusion rate constants for (c) CO<sub>2</sub> (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0–0.2) and (d) Ar (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0–0.02) through the triangular windows (<sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub>) in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue), respectively. Diffusion rate constants for (e) CO<sub>2</sub> (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0–0.2) and (f) Ar (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0–0.02) within the polyhedral pores (<sup>p</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub>) in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue), respectively.</p></div></div><p>As described in the introductory part, the dipole moment of <i>cis</i>-azobenzene is approximately 3.1 D, whereas that of its <i>trans</i> isomer is nearly zero.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(6)</a> Hence, <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> likely provides more polar crystalline channels than <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> does. Because of its quadrupole moment and polarizability, CO<sub>2</sub> may possibly interact with polar motifs by a dipole/quadrupole interaction,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(12)</a> so that <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> with a higher <i>cis</i>-isomer content likely interacts with CO<sub>2</sub> more preferentially than <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup>. To support this hypothesis, we used erythrosine B (EB) as a guest molecule to explore the change in the interior polarity of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF as a function of the <i>cis</i>-isomer content. EB is known to serve as a polarity-sensitive probe and exhibits a red shift in its absorption when located in a less polar environment.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(13)</a> Thus, EB-containing <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup> (<sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup>⊃EB) was prepared according to the procedure in <a class="ref" href="#fig4">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig4">4</a></a>a, where the amount of EB loaded in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup>, as estimated by electronic absorption spectroscopy, was 0.5 wt % (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). Upon 2 h irradiation of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup>⊃EB with visible light (420–480 nm), the diffuse reflectance spectroscopy clearly showed a red shift in a range from 470 to 600 nm (<a class="ref" href="#fig4">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig4">4</a></a>b). Note that neither <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup> without EB (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>) nor EB alone (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) showed any appreciable red shift upon irradiation with visible light.</p><div class="figure" id="fig4"><a title="Open Figure Viewer" href="#" class="thumbnail showFiguresEvent"><img alt="figure" src="/appl/literatum/publisher/achs/journals/content/jacsat/0/jacsat.ahead-of-print/jacs.7b02979/20170626/images/small/ja-2017-02979y_0004.gif" /><span class="overlay"></span></a><div class="caption hlFld-FigureCaption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Schematic representation of the procedure for including EB into <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup> (<sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup>⊃EB): Powdery <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup> was immersed in an EtOH solution of EB for 12 h in the dark, isolated by filtration, rinsed with EtOH, and then vacuum-dried in the dark. The mole ratio of incorporated EB relative to <sup>Azo</sup><i>L</i><sup>2–</sup> was 1/330. (b) Diffuse reflectance spectra of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>20%</sup>⊃EB before (blue) and after (black) the irradiation with visible light for 2 h at 25 °C.</p></div></div><p><sup>Azo</sup>MOF adopts only a face-to-face heterotropic connection between the tetrahedral and octahedral pores by sharing a triangular window (<a class="ref" href="#fig2">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig2">2</a></a>), where the diffusion event through the triangular window is likely more sluggish than those in the polyhedral pores. Furthermore, because the azobenzene pendants are localized on the window frames (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), the diffusion event through the window might be more influenced by the isomer ratio of the azobenzene pendants. Hence, for the kinetic analysis of the adsorption profiles in <a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>a,b, we used the following double-exponential equation featuring <sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> and <sup>p</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> as variables, which are supposed to be the diffusion rate constants of gaseous molecules through the triangular windows and within the polyhedral pores, respectively:<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(14)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/appl/literatum/publisher/achs/journals/content/jacsat/0/jacsat.ahead-of-print/jacs.7b02979/20170626/images/ja-2017-02979y_m001.gif" alt="" /><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>where <i>M</i><sub><i>t</i></sub> and <i>M</i><sub>e</sub> represent the amounts of gas molecules adsorbed at time <i>t</i>, and equilibrium, respectively; whereas <i>A</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>A</i><sub>2</sub> denote relative contributions of the triangular window and polyhedral pores to the overall diffusion process, with <i>A</i><sub>1</sub> + <i>A</i><sub>2</sub> = 1. As summarized in <a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S2–S5</a>, fitting each kinetic profile using forgoing equation gave a sequence of values for two diffusion rate constants <sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> and <sup>p</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). As expected, irrespective of the <i>cis</i>-isomer content of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF, the <sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> values for both CO<sub>2</sub> (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>c,e) and Ar (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>d,f) are much smaller than the corresponding <sup>p</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> values in the whole range of <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>. Of particular interest, the <sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> values for CO<sub>2</sub> in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue) are 1 order of magnitude smaller than those in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>c). In sharp contrast, the <sup>p</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> values for CO<sub>2</sub> in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (blue) and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (black) are almost identical to one another (<a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>e). Namely, the <i>cis</i>-isomer content in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF influences mostly on the diffusion event through the triangular window. Meanwhile, as shown in <a class="ref" href="#fig3">Figure <a class="ref" href="#fig3">3</a></a>d,f, such a site-specific effect of the azobenzene isomer content on the diffusion process (black and blue) was not observed for Ar possibly due to the lack of permanent dipole and quadrupole moment. A lower polarizability of Ar than CO<sub>2</sub> might also be partly responsible for this tendency.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(9)</a> Together with the facts that the kinetic diameters of CO<sub>2</sub> and Ar are substantially identical to one another and that the azobenzene pendants are localized on the window frames (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), the marked difference in the <sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> values for CO<sub>2</sub> between <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (<sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> ≫ <sup>w</sup><i>k</i><sub>d</sub> in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup>) suggests that CO<sub>2</sub> dominantly receives a polar effect, rather than a steric effect, from the azobenzene pendants when it diffuses through the triangular windows in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF. Consistent with this observation, the isosteric heat of the adsorption of CO<sub>2</sub> in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> was estimated as 38 kJ mol<sup>–1</sup> at zero coverage, which is larger than that in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> (29 kJ mol<sup>–1</sup>) (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>), indicating a higher affinity of CO<sub>2</sub> toward <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> than <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup>. Then, here comes a question that why the amount of adsorbed CO<sub>2</sub> in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> is smaller than that in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup>. Physical properties of CO<sub>2</sub> in the condensed state, gathering via a quadrupole/quadrupole interaction,<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(15)</a> could be affected by coexisting polar groups in a congested space. In fact, surface groups in such crystalline nanochannels were reported to affect the packing mode of condensed gaseous molecules.<a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="ref">(16)</a> We suppose that this is also the case in the crystalline nanochannels of <sup>Azo</sup>MOF, where the packing mode and thus the density of CO<sub>2</sub> would change with the polarity of the channel wall, i.e., the <i>cis</i>-isomer content of the azobenzene pendants.</p><p class="last">In conclusion, we demonstrated that <sup>Azo</sup>MOF provides the first porous crystalline nanochannel that photochemically modulates the diffusion kinetics of CO<sub>2</sub> by alteration of the polar interaction with the azobenzene-appended channel wall. The use of polarity-sensitive EB, together with Ar with no quadrupole moment as a similar-sized reference to CO<sub>2</sub>, nicely supported the above conclusion. Design of MOFs with other photochromic molecules is an interesting subject worthy of further investigations.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="NLM_notes" id="notes-1"> <ul class="anchors"><li><a title="Jump to a Section" onclick="showPulldown(this); return false;" href="JavaScript:void(0);" class="anchorIMG"></a><ul style="display: none;"><li><a onclick="hidePulldown(this);" title="Top of Page" href="#top"><span>Top of Page</span></a></li><li><a onclick="hidePulldown(this);" title="Abstract" href="#Abstract"><span>Abstract</span></a></li><li><a onclick="hidePulldown(this);" title="References" href="#_i3"><span>References</span></a></li></ul></li></ul><h2 id="_i1"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979">Supporting Information</a></h2><hr /><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="http://pubs.acs.org" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/abs/10.1021/jacs.7b02979" class="ext-link">10.1021/jacs.7b02979</a>.<ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Details of the synthetic procedures for <sup>Azo</sup><i>L</i>H<sub>2</sub> and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF, and kinetic parameters of CO<sub>2</sub> and Ar in <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>1%</sup> and <sup>Azo</sup>MOF<sup>15%</sup> (<a href="http://pubs.acs.org/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b02979/suppl_file/ja7b02979_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul></p></div><div class="NLM_notes" id="NOTES-d158e1455-autogenerated"><p class="first last">The authors declare no competing financial interest.</p></div><div class="ack" id="ACK-d158e1452-autogenerated"><ul class="anchors"><li><a title="Jump to a Section" onclick="showPulldown(this); Fraser; Yaghi, Omar M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <cas:volume xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">4</cas:volume>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2858-2864</span>CODEN:
                <cas:coden xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">CSHCCN</cas:coden>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-6520</span>.

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis and structure of an azobenzene functionalized isoreticular metal-org. framework (azo-IRMOF-74-III) [Mg2(C26H16O6N2)] (6) are described and the ability to controllably release a guest from its pores in response to an external stimulus was demonstrated.  Azo-IRMOF-74-III is an isoreticular expansion of MOF-74 with an etb topol. and a 1-D hexagonal pore structure.  The structure of azo-IRMOF-74-III is analogous to that of MOF-74, as demonstrated by powder x-ray diffraction, with a surface area of 2410 m2 g-1 BET.  Each org. unit within azo-IRMOF-74-III is decorated with a photoswitchable azobenzene unit, which can be toggled between its cis and trans conformation by excitation at 408 nm.  When propidium iodide dye was loaded into the MOF, spectroscopic studies showed that no release of the luminescent dye was obsd. under ambient conditions.  Upon irradn. of the MOF at 408 nm, however, the rapid wagging motion inherent to the repetitive isomerization of the azobenzene functionality triggered the release of the dye from the pores.  This light-induced release of cargo can be modulated between an on and an off state by controlling the conformation of the azobenzene with the appropriate wavelength of light.  This report highlights the ability to capture and release small mols. and demonstrates the utility of self-contained photo-active switches located inside highly porous MOFs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9am_MiEg8UrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lis8wiH8hdnXA"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXos1yqtrY%253D&amp;md5=a46f55b0cbefddec2baa0173f6d5e19f</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit1d">(d) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Modrow<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>A.</span>; Zargarani, Dordaneh; Herges, Rainer; Stock, Norbert</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Dalton Transactions</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <cas:volume xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">41</cas:volume>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">28</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8690-8696</span>CODEN:
                <cas:coden xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">DTARAF</cas:coden>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">For the first time an azo functionality was covalently introduced into a MOF by post-synthetic modification.  The reaction of Cr-MIL-101-NH2, [Cr3(μ3-O)(OH)(H2O)2(O2CC6NH5CO2)3]·nH2O, with p-phenylazobenzoylchloride (1) and 4-(phenylazo)Ph isocyanate (2) as the reactants led to the compds. Cr-MIL-101amide and Cr-MIL-101urea, with the azo groups protruding into the mesoporous cages.  XRPD and N2 sorption measurements confirm the intactness of the framework and the successful covalent modification was proven by IR- and NMR-spectroscopy.  Also, cis/trans isomerization upon irradn. with light was demonstrated by UV/visible spectroscopy.  More distinct changes in the UV/visible spectra were obsd. for Cr-MIL-101amide compared to Cr-MIL-101urea, while the degree of functionalization, i.e. the no. of reacted NH2-groups, seems to have a less pronounced effect.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The conversion of azobenzene to the 4,4'-dicarboxylate (AzDC) delivers a ligand that can be incorporated into MOF architectures.  The pillar ligand trans-1,2-bis(4-pyridyl)ethylene (4,4'-BPE) has also been reported to demonstrate cis-trans photoisomerizability when coordinated to a metal complex.  Combination of these 2 ligands within Zn-based MOFs generates the triply interpenetrated framework Zn(AzDC)(4,4'-BPE)0.5, which exhibits an open topol. amenable to high capacity and selective adsorption of H and CO2.  The triply interpenetrated framework Zn(AzDC)(4,4'BPE)0.5 was prepd. solvothermally using previously reported methods.  We report the discovery of strong, low-energy photoresponse in Zn(AzDC)(4,4'-BPE)0.5 and find that the behavior is dynamic and localized, irresp. of the use of broadband or filtered light sources.  Previous researches have not detected cis-trans changes where azo groups are part of the MOF wall and not as pendant groups in the pores.  In this article it is reported that bending about the azo group can be obsd. to occur on a local scale in a dynamic fashion, perfect for online adsorbent regeneration.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-org. frameworks offer tremendous potential for efficient sepn. of mol. mixts.  Different pore sizes and suitable functionalizations of the framework allow for an adjustment of the static selectivity.  Here we report membranes which offer dynamic control of the selectivity by remote signals, thus enabling a continuous adjustment of the permeate flux.  This is realized by assembling linkers contg. photoresponsive azobenzene-side-groups into monolithic, cryst. membranes of metal-org. frameworks.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Reaction of the microporous metal-org. framework Zn4O(BDC)3 (BDC2- = 1,4-benzenedicarboxylate) with Cr(CO)6 at 140 °C in a 6:1 mixt. of di-Bu ether and THF affords Zn4O[(BDC)Cr(CO)3]3 (1).  This compd. retains the porous cubic structure of the parent framework, but features Cr(CO)3 groups attached in an η6 fashion to all of the benzene rings.  Compd. 1 is also microporous, exhibiting a BET surface area of 2130 m2/g.  It can be fully decarbonylated by heating at 200° C, but the resulting gray solid (2) shows little affinity for N2 or H2 at 298 K, suggesting aggregation of the chromium atoms.  In contrast, photolysis of 1 using 450-nm light in an atm. of N2 or H2 produces solids with IR spectra indicative of Zn4O[(BDC)Cr(CO)2(N2)]3 (3) and Zn4O[(BDC)Cr(CO)2(H2)]3 (4).  Under an N2 atmosphere, compd. 4 completely converts into compd. 3 over the course of 12 h, demonstrating the lability of the Cr0-H2 bond.  Owing to isolation of the metal centers within the rigid, evacuable framework structures, the N2- and H2-substituted compds. show greatly enhanced stability relative to mol. analogs generated in frozen gas matrixes or supercrit. fluid solns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpsVXY1RZVrQrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lid4LpnlyO_oQ"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXhsVOnur3J&amp;md5=85a5ab8f94a2f3803a4c68446cad6718</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit5b">(b) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Jones<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>C. L.; Tansell, A. J.; Easun, T. L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <cas:volume xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">4</cas:volume>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs) have emerged over the past two decades as highly promising materials in the gas storage and sepn. arenas, with the potential to act as rapid uptake/rapid release sorbents for CO2, CH4 and H2 that may have significant impact in energy and sustainability technologies.  However, a small but growing subset of the MOF community have been developing alternative, light-induced applications of MOFs.  This review briefly outlines some of these exciting diversions from the 'traditional' applications of MOFs and focuses particularly on the design strategies of those frameworks that undergo photoinduced structural change.  These strategies are classified as either (i) the imposition of photoresponsivity by a photoresponsive guest; (ii) post-synthetic modification (PSM) of frameworks to add in photoresponsive groups; (iii) synthesis of MOFs with linkers that support pendant photoresponsive groups; and, perhaps the most challenging, (iv) synthesis of MOFs from linkers that themselves have intrinsic structural photoresponsivity such that their structure is altered on illumination.  Examples are given of each approach, future applications are proposed, and strategic pathways to next-generation photoresponsive frameworks are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrP-bOxMMWJ2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lid4LpnlyO_oQ"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitVCqsrvO&amp;md5=7c415f2f86d56c5836b7419cba58e532</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit5c">(c) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Blake<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal-org. frameworks, typically built by bridging metal centers with org. linkers, have recently shown great promise for a wide variety of applications, including gas sepn. and drug delivery. Small org. mols., capable of undergoing efficient and reversible photochem. reactions to switch them between (at least) two (meta)stable isomers assocd. with markedly different properties, continue to impact the materials world.  Such photoswitches are being implemented in a variety of materials for applications ranging from optical devices to "smart" polymers.  All approaches exploit the photoswitching mol. entities as gates, which translate an incoming light stimulus to trigger macroscopic property changes of the materials.  In this progress report, the most promising recent examples in this field are highlighted and put in perspective.         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Porous crystals are strategic materials with industrial applications within petrochemistry, catalysis, gas storage, and selective separation.  Their unique properties are based on the molecular-scale porous character.  However, a principal limitation of zeolites and similar oxide-based materials is the relatively small size of the pores, typically in the range of medium-sized molecules, limiting their use in pharmaceutical and fine chemical applications.  Metal organic frameworks (MOFs) provided a breakthrough in this respect.  New MOFs appear at a high and an increasing pace, but the appearances of new, stable inorganic building bricks are rare.  Here we present a new zirconium-based inorganic building brick that allows the synthesis of very high surface area MOFs with unprecedented stability.  The high stability is based on the combination of strong Zr-O bonds and the ability of the inner Zr6-cluster to rearrange reversibly upon removal or addition of mu3-OH groups, without any changes in the connecting carboxylates.  The weak thermal, chemical, and mechanical stability of most MOFs is probably the most important property that limits their use in large scale industrial applications.  The Zr-MOFs presented in this work have the toughness needed for industrial applications; decomposition temperature above 500 degrees C and resistance to most chemicals, and they remain crystalline even after exposure to 10 tons/cm2 of external pressure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRKYQUu8sD86Q8IxFYvehhofW6udTcc2eaYSTXjbAx9QLntkvexoksv"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BD1cnlvFSqsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=f355e3a0756d112f6ae3148a1e69cd81</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="JavaScript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9');return false;" class="refNumLink">9. </a></strong><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit9"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Li<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>J.-R.</span>; <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Kuppler<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>R.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Adsorptive sepn. is very important in industry.  Generally, the process uses porous solid materials such as zeolites, activated carbons, or silica gels as adsorbents.  With an ever increasing need for a more efficient, energy-saving, and environmentally benign procedure for gas sepn., adsorbents with tailored structures and tunable surface properties must be found.  Metal-org. frameworks (MOFs), constructed by metal-contg. nodes connected by org. bridges, are such a new type of porous materials.  They are promising candidates as adsorbents for gas sepns. due to their large surface areas, adjustable pore sizes and controllable properties, as well as acceptable thermal stability.  This crit. review starts with a brief introduction to gas sepn. and purifn. based on selective adsorption, followed by a review of gas selective adsorption in rigid and flexible MOFs.  Based on possible mechanisms, selective adsorptions obsd. in MOFs are classified, and primary relationships between adsorption properties and framework features are analyzed.  As a specific example of tailor-made MOFs, mesh-adjustable mol. sieves are emphasized and the underlying working mechanism elucidated.  In addn. to the exptl. aspect, theor. investigations from adsorption equil. to diffusion dynamics via mol. simulations are also briefly reviewed.  Furthermore, gas sepns. in MOFs, including the mol. sieving effect, kinetic sepn., the quantum sieving effect for H2/D2 sepn., and MOF-based membranes are also summarized (227 refs.).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdWpKQXLGaW7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhO2KQuDKi4CQ"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXkvVamurY%253D&amp;md5=cbb650af8ce92526fc8ffd870218bbd0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="JavaScript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10');return false;" class="refNumLink">10. </a></strong><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit10"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Schaate<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>A.</span>;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present a study on the influence of benzoic acid, acetic acid, and water on the syntheses of the Zr-based metal-org. frameworks Zr-bdc (UiO-66), Zr-bdc-NH2 (UiO-66-NH2), Zr-bpdc (UiO-67), and Zr-tpdc-NH2 (UiO-68-NH2) (H2bdc: terephthalic acid, H2bpdc: biphenyl-4,4'-dicarboxylic acid, H2tpdc: terphenyl-4,4''-dicarboxylic acid).  By varying the amt. of benzoic or acetic acid, the synthesis of Zr-bdc can be modulated.  With increasing concn. of the modulator, the products change from intergrown to individual crystals, the size of which can be tuned.  Addn. of benzoic acid also affects the size and morphol. of Zr-bpdc and, addnl., makes the synthesis of Zr-bpdc highly reproducible.  The control of crystal and particle size is proven by powder XRD, SEM and dynamic light scattering (DLS) measurements.  TGA and Ar sorption expts. show that the materials from modulated syntheses can be activated and that they exhibit high sp. surface areas.  Water proved to be essential for the formation of well-ordered Zr-bdc-NH2.  Zr-tpdc-NH2, a material with a structure analogous to that of Zr-bdc and Zr-bpdc, but with the longer, functionalized linker 2'-amino-1,1':4',1''-terphenyl-4,4''-dicarboxylic acid, was obtained as single crystals.  This allowed the 1st single-crystal structural anal. of a Zr-based metal-org. framework.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpua8VajcCGT7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhO2KQuDKi4CQ"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXmslKksLc%253D&amp;md5=188604c14defb7b374f67d9cf2c480ea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="JavaScript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11');return false;" class="refNumLink">11. </a></strong><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit11a">(a) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Lv<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>X.-L.</span>;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Exploitation of new metal-org. framework (MOF) materials with high surface areas has been attracting great attention in related research communities due to their broad potential applications.  In this work, a new Zr(IV)-based MOF, [Zr6O4(OH)4(eddb)6] (BUT-30, H2eddb = 4,4'-(ethyne-1,2-diyl)dibenzoic acid) has been solvothermally synthesized, characterized, and explored for gases and dyes adsorptions.  Single-crystal X-ray diffraction anal. demonstrates a three-dimensional cubic framework structure of this MOF, in which each Zr6O4(OH)4 building unit is linked by 12 linear eddb ligands.  BUT-30 has been found stable up to 400°C and has a Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area as high as 3940.6 m2 g-1 (based on the N2 adsorption at 77 K) and total pore vol. of 1.55 cm3 g-1.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An extensive review is given on progress made in the capture of CO2 from power plant emissions, briefly considering also the CO2 storage (sequestration) of the enormous quantities of CO2 that would be obtained as a result of a successful capture framework.  The main existing CO2 capture technologies are discussed in the context of capture performance considerations of aq. alkanolamine solns. and porous solids such as zeolites and activated carbons.  Schematic diagrams are presented of idealized temp. swing adsorption, pressure swing adsorption, and vacuum swing adsorption processes for regenerating solid adsorbent in a fixed-bed column.  The structural features and CO2 adsorption characteristics of isoreticular metal-org. frameworks (MOF) are discussed in detail.  The high-pressure adsorption capacities of 36 MOF and the lower-pressure adsorption capacities of many more MOF, at 293-319 K, are tabulated.  The various types of functionalities that have been explored to date for prepg. materials with enhanced CO2 capture performance are outlined, including amines, strongly polarizing org. functionalities, and exposed metal cation sites.  The synthesis of continuous films of MOF and their use in membranes for gas sepn. are examd., with the tabulation of the CO2 sepn. performance of MOF mixed-matrix membranes.  A final section outlines main issues that need to be addressed to achieve next-generation materials capable of fulfilling the criteria required for the gas sepns. relevant to post-combustion CO2 capture, pre-combustion CO2 capture, and oxy-fuel combustion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoX3Kc-n2tAHrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhFJZIrln3TuA"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXhs12hsLzE&amp;md5=eb8236e7dfe12981387c71415374f958</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit12b">(b) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Song<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>C.</span>;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A systematic investigation of CO2 adsorption behavior in three metal-org. frameworks was executed.  The three MOFs adopted the same NbO-type structure, except that the org. ligands were grafted with different highly polarized heterocyclic moieties, namely, oxadiazole, thiadiazole, and selenadiazole, resp.  After activation, the three MOF materials showed different surface areas and pore vols. depending on the incorporated heterocyclic rings attached to the org. ligands as well as the MOF's stabilities.  Among the three MOF materials, ZJNU-41a exhibited an impressive CO2 uptake capacity of 97.4 cm3 (STP) g-1 at 298 K and 1 atm, which is comparable and even superior to those reported in NbO-type MOFs.  In particular, when the mol. dipole of the attached heterocyclic moieties increases, the CO2 uptake also increases, which was further supported by comprehensive quantum chem. calcns.  This work demonstrates that the introduction of highly polarized heterocyclic functional groups into frameworks is a promising approach to target porous metal-org. framework materials with improved CO2 adsorption performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo-cWFpiB6jqbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liWZanMtBiA0w"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVSksL7J&amp;md5=22f3b8fe180d30c21ab1a58f6eb220b7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="JavaScript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13');return false;" class="refNumLink">13. </a></strong><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit13"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Otani<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>W.</span>;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A mesostructured silica/org. composite 1-MS, constructed from a rodlike micelle of amino acid amphiphile I that has a condensable head group and that can be used as a template, was found to be able to catalyze the acetalization of cyclohexanone, in ethanol at 25°C (50% in 12h), whereas no reaction took place with unfunctionalized mesoporous silica.  In sharp contrast, hydrolytic removal of the C16 alkyl tail of immobilized I resulted in the complete disappearance of the catalytic activity, which suggests the importance of a hydrophobic inner domain for the admission of cyclohexanone.  Unsupported peptide amphiphile II, under identical conditions to those above, was inefficient for acetalization regardless of the absence (2% in 24 h) or presence of mesoporous silica (7% in 24 h).  Ref. composite II-MS, which is a noncovalently immobilized peptidic micelle, was virtually inactive (1% in 24 h).  These observations indicate the importance of covalent immobilization of the peptidic rod micelle for catalysis.  Mesostructured silicate III-MS hybridized with a nonpeptidic, ammonium ion amphiphile (III) showed a certain catalytic activity, but the yield (12% in 24 h) of the acetal was much lower than that achieved by using I-MS as the catalyst.  Amorphous silica with immobilized I on its surface was much less active than I-MS for acetalization (5% in 24 h).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrF8-9JPeoDjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liWZanMtBiA0w"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXitlartLk%253D&amp;md5=1076bcdd69ac1c2d027f14de4aaafd44</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="JavaScript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14');return false;" class="refNumLink">14. </a></strong><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit14a">(a) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Choi<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>H.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <cas:volume xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">130</cas:volume>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Reaction of Co(CF3SO3)2 with the new mol. 1,4-benzenedi(4'-pyrazolyl) (H2BDP) in N,N'-diethylformamide (DEF) at 130 °C generates the metal-org. framework Co(BDP)·2DEF·H2O (1).  X-ray anal. reveals the structure of 1 to contain chains of tetrahedrally ligated Co2+ ions linked through BDP2- ligands to generate a three-dimensional framework with 10 × 10 Å2 channels.  Thermogravimetric data shows the framework to have a high thermal stability, and complete desolvation occurs upon heating at 170 °C under dynamic vacuum for two days to afford 1d.  X-ray powder diffraction data indicates that 1d possesses a substantially different structure, but converts back to 1 upon exposure to DEF, consistent with the presence of a flexible framework.  Nitrogen adsorption isotherms measured for 1d at 77 and 87 K reveal an unprecedented five-step adsorption process and a Langmuir surface area of 2670 m2/g.  In addn., high-pressure H2 adsorption data reveal hysteretic uptake and release, with hysteresis loops of width 1.1, 3.8, 13, and 27 bar that shift to higher pressures as the temp. increases from 50 to 65, 77, and 87 K, resp.  The high H2 uptake capacity of 5.5 excess wt % at 50 K suggests that such materials could potentially find utility for hydrogen storage via a kinetic trapping mechanism.  Variable-temp. kinetics measurements have also allowed the first study of H2 diffusion within a metal-org. framework, revealing an energy barrier of 0.62 kJ/mol for H2 diffusing within the pores.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpaKKakmE2c5rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liWZanMtBiA0w"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXms1Gitb0%253D&amp;md5=6355a96c062de540c55aa13afe969ba5</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit14b">(b) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Fletcher<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>A.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Desolvation of Ni2(4,4'-bipyridine)3(NO3)4·2CH3OH and Ni2(4,4'-bipyridine)3(NO3)4·2C2H5OH give flexible metal-org. porous structures M and E, resp., which have the same stoichiometry, but subtly different structures.  This study combines measurements of the thermodn. and kinetics of carbon dioxide, methanol, and ethanol sorption on adsorbents M and E over a range of temps. with adsorbent structural characterization at different adsorbate (guest) loadings.  The adsorption kinetics for methanol and ethanol adsorption on porous structure E obey a linear driving force (LDF) mass transfer model for adsorption at low surface coverage.  The corresponding adsorption kinetics for porous structure M follow a double exponential (DE) model, which is consistent with two different barriers for diffusion through the windows and along the pores in the structure.  The former is a high-energy barrier due to the opening of the windows in the structure, required to allow adsorption to occur, while the latter is a lower-energy barrier for diffusion in the pore cavities.  X-ray diffraction studies at various methanol and ethanol loadings showed that the host porous structures E and M underwent different scissoring motions, leading to an increase in unit cell vol. with the space group remaining unchanged during adsorption.  The results are discussed in terms of reversible adsorbate/adsorbent (host/guest) structural changes and the adsorption mechanism involving hydrogen-bonding interactions with sp. surface sites for methanol and ethanol adsorption in relation to pore size and extent of filling.  This paper contains the first evidence for individual kinetic barriers to diffusion through windows and pore cavities in flexible porous coordination polymer frameworks.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrcSPjxUlxM9rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liWZanMtBiA0w"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2cXlvVSktrs%253D&amp;md5=8411f5a8659801221ba73a7b0b76df57</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="JavaScript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15');return false;" class="refNumLink">15. </a></strong><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit15a">(a) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Santoro<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>M.</span>; <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Gorelli<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>F.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon capture combined with utilization and storage has the potential to serve as a near-term option for CO2 emissions redn.  CO2 capture by carbon-based sorbents and CO2 storage in geol. formations such as coal and shale both require a thorough understanding of the CO2 adsorption properties in microporous carbon-based materials.  Complex pore structures for natural org. materials, such as coal and gas shale, in addn. to general carbon-based porous materials are modeled as a collection of independent, noninterconnected, functionalized graphitic slit pores with surface heterogeneities.  Electronic structure calcns. coupled with van der Waals-inclusive corrections have been performed to investigate the electronic properties of functionalized graphitic surfaces.  With Bader charge anal., electronic structure calcns. can provide the initial framework comprising both the geometry and corresponding charge information required to carry out statistical modeling.  Grand canonical Monte Carlo simulations were carried out to det. the adsorption isotherms for a given adsorbent-adsorbate interaction at temp./pressure conditions relevant to carbon capture applications to focus on the effect of the surface functionalities. Chem.</span>  <span class="NLM_year">1996</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume">100</span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">9006</span>, <span class="refDoi">DOI: 10.1021/jp953393a</span> <div class="citationLinks">[<a href="/doi/10.1021/jp953393a" title="">ACS Full Text <img src="/templates/jsp/_style2/_achs/images/acs-ref.gif" alt="ACS Full Text" /></a>], [<a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=ref16/cit16c&amp;dbid=32&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.7b02979&amp;key=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK28Xis1yitrY%253D" title="">CAS</a></a>]<div class="casRecord" id="cas_cit16cR"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16. </span><div class="casTitle"><cas:atitle xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">Molecular Simulation Study of the Structural Rearrangement of Methane Adsorbed in Aluminophosphate AlPO4-5</cas:atitle></div><div class="casAuthors">Lachet, Veronique; Boutin, Anne; Pellenq, Roland J.-M.; Nicholson, David; Fuchs, Alain</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <cas:volume xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">100</cas:volume>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9006-13</span>CODEN:
                <cas:coden xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">JPCHAX</cas:coden>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-3654</span>.

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Grand canonical Monte Carlo simulations were performed for CH4 adsorbed in the aluminophosphate AlPO4 5.  Several approaches were used to model the methane-methane as well as the methane-adsorbent interactions.  A potential function of the Pellenq and Nicholson (PN) type (which includes 2-body, 3-body dispersion terms, induced interactions, and exponential repulsions with each individual framework species) can describe adequately the exptl. obsd. substep in the adsorption isotherms.  The correspondence between the calcd. and exptl. adsorption isotherms (assuming a rigid microporous crystal) confirms that the obsd. substep may be attributed to a structural rearrangement of the adsorbed phase, similar in nature to a phase transition.  Good agreement was also found between the simulated and exptl. isosteric heats of adsorption curves.  The pressure at which the step is obsd. in the simulations depends crucially on the value of the repulsive O-methane parameter.  Two structures of the adsorbed phase with max. loading were found, by using slightly different values of this latter parameter.  Calcd. neutron diffraction spectra for both structures agree fairly well with expts.  These 2 phases are presumably close to each other in energy and stability and further expts. are needed to conclude on the exact nature of the confined CH4 structure at high pressure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr0YNA_iW0Q-bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgfq35i5I05vA"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK28Xis1yitrY%253D&amp;md5=4b16ad6138fb75cb1ef8866fe2ed6343</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_citation" id="cit16d">(d) <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Kitagawa<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>S.</span>; <span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor ">Matsuda<span class="NLM_x"><x xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xml:space="preserve">, </x></span>R.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Coord. Chem. Rev.</span>  <span class="NLM_year">2007</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume">251</span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2490</span>, <span class="refDoi">DOI: 10.1016/j.ccr.2007.07.009</span> <div class="citationLinks">[<a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=ref16/cit16d&amp;dbid=16&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.7b02979&amp;key=10.1016%2Fj.ccr.2007.07.009" title="">CrossRef</a>], [<a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=ref16/cit16d&amp;dbid=32&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.7b02979&amp;key=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXhtFKhs77L" title="">CAS</a></a>]<div class="casRecord" id="cas_cit16dR"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16. </span><div class="casTitle"><cas:atitle xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">Chemistry of coordination space of porous coordination polymers</cas:atitle></div><div class="casAuthors">Kitagawa, Susumu; Matsuda, Ryotaro</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Coordination Chemistry Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <cas:volume xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">251</cas:volume>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21-24</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2490-2509</span>CODEN:
                <cas:coden xmlns:cas="http://www.cas.org/citations" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:ACS="http://namespace.acs.org/2008/acs" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance">CCHRAM</cas:coden>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0010-8545</span>.

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Recently, syntheses of epoch-making space materials using coordination compds. are underway by various groups.  The rational synthetic strategy of the space of coordination compds. provides unique structures and functions, which were not found in traditional materials.  Now, the authors intend to focus on this scientific field from a new viewpoint.  The authors define coordination space as the space where the coordination bond plays an important role in the formation of the spatial structures and where various phys. properties are exhibited.  The authors focus on the coordination spaces provided by porous coordination polymers, and their uniqueness is illustrated with current representative results.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder &#174;" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqiHF8NDmDVp7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgfq35i5I05vA"> >> More from SciFinder <sup>&#174;</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXhtFKhs77L&amp;md5=1552e3eeb4989b9f8c862048f86e7094</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div></div></div><div id="figshare-widget"></div><script type="text/javascript">

These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.<br /><br />The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. <script type="text/javascript">
$(function(){
	$('.altmetric-toggle').click(function(){
		var button = $('.altmetric-toggle');
		var buttonText = $('.altmetric-toggle-text');
		if ($(buttonText).text() == 'More Article Metrics') {
			$(button).addClass('open');
			$(buttonText).text('Hide More Article Metrics');
			$('#toggler').addClass('toggle-rotate');
			$('.metrics-main').slideUp();
			$('.altmetric-wrap').slideDown();
		}
		else {
			$(button).removeClass('open');
			$(buttonText).text('More Article Metrics');
			$('#toggler').removeClass('toggle-rotate');
			$('.metrics-main').slideDown();
			$('.altmetric-wrap').slideUp();
		}
	});
});
</script>
